<p><a href="/wiki/Welsh_Assembly" class="mw-redirect" title="Welsh Assembly">Welsh Assembly</a> elections normally occur every four years. They elect the <a href="/wiki/Members_of_the_National_Assembly_for_Wales" class="mw-redirect" title="Members of the National Assembly for Wales">Members of the National Assembly for Wales</a> (AMs). They began in 1999, when the <a href="/wiki/Unicameral" class="mw-redirect" title="Unicameral">unicameral</a> Welsh Assembly, created by the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Wales_Act_1998" title="Government of Wales Act 1998">Government of Wales Act 1998</a>, began its first session. However AMs voted to hold the most recent election in 2016 to avoid a clash with the UK parliamentary general election in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> For elections to the Welsh Assembly the <a href="/wiki/Additional_Member_System" title="Additional Member System">Additional Member System</a> is used, which is a hybrid of <a href="/wiki/Single_member_plurality" class="mw-redirect" title="Single member plurality">single member plurality</a> and <a href="/wiki/Proportional_representation" title="Proportional representation">proportional representation</a>.
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Welsh_Assembly_Election,_1999" class="mw-redirect" title="Welsh Assembly Election, 1999">Welsh Assembly Election 1999</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Welsh_Assembly_Election,_2003" class="mw-redirect" title="Welsh Assembly Election, 2003">Welsh Assembly Election 2003</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_for_Wales_election,_2007" title="National Assembly for Wales election, 2007">Welsh Assembly Election 2007</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_for_Wales_election,_2011" title="National Assembly for Wales election, 2011">Welsh Assembly Election 2011</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_for_Wales_election,_2016" title="National Assembly for Wales election, 2016">Welsh Assembly Election 2016</a></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Northern_Ireland_Assembly_elections">Northern Ireland Assembly elections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elections_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Northern Ireland Assembly elections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Assembly" title="Northern Ireland Assembly">Northern Ireland Assembly</a> elections occur every four years on the first Thursday in May. They began in 1998, when the assembly created by the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Act_1998" title="Northern Ireland Act 1998">Northern Ireland Act 1998</a> began its first session. For elections to the Northern Ireland Assembly, the <a href="/wiki/Single_Transferable_Vote" class="mw-redirect" title="Single Transferable Vote">Single Transferable Vote system</a>, is used.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Under this system, voters rank individual candidates in order of preference. STV was chosen as the electoral method to attempt to give adequate representation to the different <a href="/wiki/Sectarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Sectarian">sectarian</a> groups in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>. Elections continued even when the assembly was suspended between 2002 and 2007.
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Assembly_election,_1998" title="Northern Ireland Assembly election, 1998">Northern Ireland Assembly election 1998</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Assembly_election,_2003" title="Northern Ireland Assembly election, 2003">Northern Ireland Assembly election 2003</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Assembly_election,_2007" title="Northern Ireland Assembly election, 2007">Northern Ireland Assembly election 2007</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Assembly_election,_2011" title="Northern Ireland Assembly election, 2011">Northern Ireland Assembly election 2011</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Assembly_election,_2016" title="Northern Ireland Assembly election, 2016">Northern Ireland Assembly election 2016</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Assembly_election,_2017" title="Northern Ireland Assembly election, 2017">Northern Ireland Assembly election 2017</a></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="European_Parliament_elections">European Parliament elections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elections_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: European Parliament elections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Elections to the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament" title="European Parliament">European Parliament</a> have taken place since <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament_election,_1979_(United_Kingdom)" title="European Parliament election, 1979 (United Kingdom)">1979</a>, the first year in which the parliament was directly elected. (From 1973 to 1979, members were elected by national parliaments).
</p><p>Since the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament_election,_1999_(United_Kingdom)" title="European Parliament election, 1999 (United Kingdom)">1999 election</a>, <a href="/wiki/Members_of_the_European_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="Members of the European Parliament">Members of the European Parliament</a> (MEPs) representing England, Scotland and Wales have been elected in regional constituencies using the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Closed_Party_List_system&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Closed Party List system (page does not exist)">party list</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Closed_list" title="Closed list">closed list</a> (i.e. candidates are chosen by parties). In <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> the <a href="/wiki/Single_Transferable_Vote" class="mw-redirect" title="Single Transferable Vote">Single Transferable Vote system</a> has been used since <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament_election,_1979_(United_Kingdom)" title="European Parliament election, 1979 (United Kingdom)">1979</a>.
</p><p>The United Kingdom is divided into twelve electoral regions, which are the three smaller nations (<a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>), and nine <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_England" title="Regions of England">Regions of England</a>.
</p><p>The use of <a href="/wiki/Proportional_representation" title="Proportional representation">proportional representation</a> greatly increased the representation of minor parties. Until the 1999 election, the First Past the Post system was used, which had prevented parties with moderately large, but geographically spread out vote shares from receiving any seats. For example, in the 1989 election the <a href="/wiki/Green_Party_of_England_and_Wales" title="Green Party of England and Wales">Green Party</a> received 2,292,718 votes, constituting a 15% vote share, but no seats. The <a href="/wiki/European_Parliamentary_Elections_Act_1999" title="European Parliamentary Elections Act 1999">European Parliamentary Elections Act 1999</a> changed the system in time for the 1999 election.
</p><p>From 1979 to 1989, the United Kingdom had 81 <a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_European_Parliament" title="Member of the European Parliament">MEPs</a> (78 in England, Wales and Scotland, 3 in Northern Ireland). The <a href="/wiki/European_Parliamentary_Elections_Act_1993" title="European Parliamentary Elections Act 1993">European Parliamentary Elections Act 1993</a> increased the number to 87, adding five more seats in England and one more in Wales. The number was reduced to 78 for the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament_election,_2004_(United_Kingdom)" title="European Parliament election, 2004 (United Kingdom)">2004 election</a>, and to 72 for the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament_election,_2009_(United_Kingdom)" title="European Parliament election, 2009 (United Kingdom)">2009 election</a>, but increased to 73 during the term of the 2009-2014 parliament. The UK's representation in Europe remained at this level in <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament_election,_2014_(United_Kingdom)" title="European Parliament election, 2014 (United Kingdom)">2014</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_and_local_elections">Regional and local elections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elections_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Regional and local elections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Administrative_geography_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Administrative geography of the United Kingdom">Administrative geography of the United Kingdom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Local_government_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Local government in the United Kingdom">Local government in the United Kingdom</a></div>
<p>In local elections, councillors are elected forming the local administrations of the United Kingdom. A number of tiers of local council exist, at <a href="/wiki/Region" title="Region">region</a>, <a href="/wiki/County" title="County">county</a>, district/<a href="/wiki/Borough" title="Borough">borough</a> and town/<a href="/wiki/Civil_parish_(England)" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil parish (England)">parish</a> levels. A variety of voting systems are used for local elections. In <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Single_transferable_vote" title="Single transferable vote">single transferable vote</a> system is used, whilst in most of <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> the <a href="/wiki/First_Past_the_Post_electoral_system" class="mw-redirect" title="First Past the Post electoral system">single member plurality system</a> is used. The remainder of England (including all of the <a href="/wiki/London_Boroughs" class="mw-redirect" title="London Boroughs">London Boroughs</a>) and Wales use the <a href="/wiki/Plurality-at-large_voting" title="Plurality-at-large voting">plurality at-large</a> system, except for the elections of the Mayor and Assembly of the <a href="/wiki/Greater_London_Authority" title="Greater London Authority">Greater London Authority</a> (GLA).
</p><p>The only Region of England which has a directly elected administration is London. <a href="/wiki/London_Assembly" title="London Assembly">London Assembly</a> elections began in 2000, when it was created. The <a href="/wiki/Additional_Member_System" title="Additional Member System">Additional Member System</a> is used for elections to the Assembly. The Mayor is elected via the <a href="/wiki/Supplementary_Vote" class="mw-redirect" title="Supplementary Vote">Supplementary Vote</a> system.
</p><p>Local elections are held in different parts of the country each year. In general, local elections are held on the first Thursday in May.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> In years with a <a href="/wiki/General_election" title="General election">general election</a> it is usual practice to hold both general and local elections on the same day. In 2004, for the first time, local elections were held on the same day as <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament_Election,_2004_(UK)" class="mw-redirect" title="European Parliament Election, 2004 (UK)">European elections</a>, and London <a href="/wiki/London_mayoral_election,_2004" title="London mayoral election, 2004">Mayoral</a> and <a href="/wiki/London_Assembly_election,_2004" title="London Assembly election, 2004">Assembly</a> elections. The date was referred to as '<a href="/wiki/Super_Thursday" class="mw-redirect" title="Super Thursday">Super Thursday</a>'.
</p><p>Unlike general elections, for local elections there is no legal requirement as to when the count should begin after the close of poll.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> For this reason, some <a href="/wiki/Returning_Officer#United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Returning Officer">returning officers</a> have decided to store the sealed ballot boxes overnight at the central counting location and begin the count the next working day. However, once the count has started, the <a href="/wiki/Returning_Officer#United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Returning Officer">returning officer</a> must, so far as practicable, proceed continuously with the count between the hours of 9am and 7pm (subject to refreshments).<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Ballot papers are verified manually and counted by hand (with the exception of <a href="/wiki/London_mayoral_elections" title="London mayoral elections">London Mayoral and Assembly elections</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Optical_scan_voting_system" title="Optical scan voting system">optical scanners</a> are used).<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Police_and_Crime_Commissioners">Police and Crime Commissioners</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elections_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Police and Crime Commissioners">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>From 2012, <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> have voted for regional <a href="/wiki/Police_and_Crime_Commissioner_(England_and_Wales)" class="mw-redirect" title="Police and Crime Commissioner (England and Wales)">Police and Crime Commissioners</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elections_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Parliament_in_the_Making#Historical_events_in_the_development_of_Parliament" title="Parliament in the Making">Parliament in the Making §&#160;Historical events in the development of Parliament</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Before_the_creation_of_the_United_Kingdom">Before the creation of the United Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elections_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Before the creation of the United Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">Kingdom of England</a> (of which Wales was a <a href="/wiki/Laws_in_Wales_Acts_1535_and_1542" title="Laws in Wales Acts 1535 and 1542">full and equal member from 1542</a>), a few percent of the adult male population were able to vote in <a href="/wiki/List_of_Parliaments_of_England" title="List of Parliaments of England">parliamentary elections</a> that occurred at irregular intervals to the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">Parliament of England</a> from 1265.<sup id="cite_ref-Origins_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Origins-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> From 1432 only <a href="/wiki/Forty-shilling_freeholders" title="Forty-shilling freeholders">forty-shilling freeholders</a> held the parliamentary franchise. The franchise for the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Scotland" title="Parliament of Scotland">Parliament of Scotland</a> developed separately but, again, involving just a small proportion of the adult population. The <a href="/wiki/Bill_of_Rights_1689" title="Bill of Rights 1689">Bill of Rights 1689</a> in England and <a href="/wiki/Claim_of_Right_Act_1689" title="Claim of Right Act 1689">Claim of Right Act 1689</a> in Scotland established the principles of regular parliaments and free elections,<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> but no significant changes to the electoral franchise had taken place by the time the United Kingdom had come into being.
</p><p>Similarly, the <a href="/wiki/History_of_local_government_in_England" title="History of local government in England">history of local government in England</a> stretches over the same period with the election of town mayors and the development of town councils taking place since the Middle Ages. Local government <a href="/wiki/History_of_local_government_in_Scotland" title="History of local government in Scotland">in Scotland</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_local_government_in_Wales" title="History of local government in Wales">in Wales</a> evolved separately.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Expansion_of_the_franchise">Expansion of the franchise</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elections_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Expansion of the franchise">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The system of <a href="/wiki/Universal_suffrage" title="Universal suffrage">universal suffrage</a> did not exist in the United Kingdom until 1928.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> From the birth of the United Kingdom, the franchise had been restricted to males by custom rather than statute;<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> on rare occasions women had been able to vote in parliamentary elections as a result of property ownership until the 1832 <a href="/wiki/Reform_Act_1832" title="Reform Act 1832">Great Reform Act</a> specified 'male persons'.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first Act to increase the size of the electorate was the <a href="/wiki/Reform_Act_1832" title="Reform Act 1832">Reform Act 1832</a> (sometimes known as the Great Reform Act). It abolished 56 <a href="/wiki/Rotten_borough" class="mw-redirect" title="Rotten borough">rotten boroughs</a> (which had elected 112 MPs) and decreased the property qualification in boroughs. It gave some parliamentary representation to the industrial towns (142 MPs) by redistributing some MPs from boroughs who had disproportional representation. The electoral register was created. The overall result of the Act was that the electorate was increased to 14% of the adult male population. Although this was not a large increase, the Act was the first big step towards equal representation.
</p><p>Between 1838 and 1848 a popular movement, <a href="/wiki/Chartism" title="Chartism">Chartism</a>, organised around six demands including universal male franchise and the secret ballot.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Reform_Act_1867" title="Reform Act 1867">Reform Act 1867</a> redistributed more MPs from boroughs which had disproportional representation (42) to London and industrial towns. It decreased the property qualification in boroughs, so that all men with an address in boroughs could vote. For the first time some of the working class could vote and MPs had to take these new constituents into account. Some political parties decided to become national parties. Overall, the Act increased the size of the electorate to 32% of the adult male population.
</p><p>In local government elections, single women <a href="/wiki/Ratepayer" class="mw-redirect" title="Ratepayer">ratepayers</a> received the right to vote in the Municipal Franchise Act 1869. This right was confirmed in the <a href="/wiki/Local_Government_Act_1894" title="Local Government Act 1894">Local Government Act 1894</a> and extended to include some married women.<sup id="cite_ref-Heater2006_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heater2006-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Women&#39;s_rights_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Women&#39;s_rights-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Synonym_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Synonym-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Ballot_Act_1872" title="Ballot Act 1872">Ballot Act 1872</a> replaced open elections with a secret ballot system. The <a href="/wiki/Corrupt_and_Illegal_Practices_Prevention_Act_1883" title="Corrupt and Illegal Practices Prevention Act 1883">Corrupt and Illegal Practices Prevention Act 1883</a> criminalised attempts to bribe voters and standardised the amount that could be spent on <a href="/wiki/Election_expenses" class="mw-redirect" title="Election expenses">election expenses</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Representation_of_the_People_Act_1884" title="Representation of the People Act 1884">Representation of the People Act 1884</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Redistribution_of_Seats_Act_1885" title="Redistribution of Seats Act 1885">Redistribution of Seats Act 1885</a> (the Third Reform Act) together increased the electorate to 56% of the adult male population.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elections_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Representation_of_the_People_Act_1918" title="Representation of the People Act 1918">Representation of the People Act 1918</a> expanded the electorate to include all men over the age of 21 and most women over the age of 30. Later that year, the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_(Qualification_of_Women)_Act_1918" title="Parliament (Qualification of Women) Act 1918">Parliament (Qualification of Women) Act 1918</a> gave women over 21 the right to stand for election as MPs. The first woman to become an MP was <a href="/wiki/Constance_Markievicz" title="Constance Markievicz">Constance Markievicz</a> in 1918. However she declined to take up her seat, being a member of <a href="/wiki/Sinn_F%C3%A9in" title="Sinn Féin">Sinn Féin</a>. <a href="/wiki/Nancy_Astor,_Viscountess_Astor" title="Nancy Astor, Viscountess Astor">Nancy Astor</a>, elected in 1919, was the second woman to become an MP, and the first to sit in the Commons. The <a href="/wiki/Equal_Franchise_Act_1928" class="mw-redirect" title="Equal Franchise Act 1928">Equal Franchise Act 1928</a> lowered the minimum age for women to vote from 30 to 21, making men and women equal in terms of suffrage for the first time. The <a href="/wiki/Representation_of_the_People_Act_1949" title="Representation of the People Act 1949">Representation of the People Act 1949</a> abolished additional votes for graduates (<a href="/wiki/University_constituencies" class="mw-redirect" title="University constituencies">university constituencies</a>) and the owners of business premises.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Representation_of_the_People_Act_1969" title="Representation of the People Act 1969">Representation of the People Act 1969</a> lowered the voting age from 21 to 18. The <a href="/wiki/Representation_of_the_People_Act_1985" title="Representation of the People Act 1985">Representation of the People Act 1985</a> gave British citizens abroad the right to vote for a five-year period after they had left the United Kingdom. The <a href="/wiki/Representation_of_the_People_Act_1989" title="Representation of the People Act 1989">Representation of the People Act 1989</a> extended the period to 20 years; and citizens who were too young to vote when they left the country also became eligible.
</p><p>In the history of the electoral system there has been a clear bias. This bias has been known to the parties involved and has not been pleasing to all parties. While the bias appears to be changing, the electoral system still holds a bias. <sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Summary">Summary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elections_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Summary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The following table summarises historic developments in extending the franchise in England and later the UK (after 1707). At each stage, it shows the percentage of the adult population entitled to vote and the voting age, separately for males and females.
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center;">

<tbody><tr>

<h3><span id="Labour_.28post-1997.29_reforms"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Labour_(post-1997)_reforms">Labour (post-1997) reforms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elections_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Labour (post-1997) reforms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Prior to 1997, and the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> government of <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a>, there were only three types of elections: elections to the House of Commons, local government elections, and elections to the European Parliament. Most elections were conducted under the <a href="/wiki/First_Past_the_Post" class="mw-redirect" title="First Past the Post">First Past the Post</a> (FPTP) electoral system. In Northern Ireland, both local government and European elections were conducted under the <a href="/wiki/Single_Transferable_Vote" class="mw-redirect" title="Single Transferable Vote">Single Transferable Vote</a> (STV) system. Labour's constitutional reforms introduced elected assemblies for London, Scotland and Wales, and <a href="/wiki/Mayors_in_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Mayors in the United Kingdom">elected mayors</a> in certain cities. <a href="/wiki/Proportional_Representation" class="mw-redirect" title="Proportional Representation">Proportional Representation</a> (PR) was introduced outside Northern Ireland for the first time.
</p><p>The hybrid (part PR, part FPTP) <a href="/wiki/Additional_Member_System" title="Additional Member System">Additional Member System</a> was introduced in 1999 for the newly created devolved assemblies: the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament" title="Scottish Parliament">Scottish Parliament</a>, <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Assembly" class="mw-redirect" title="Welsh Assembly">Welsh Assembly</a> and <a href="/wiki/London_Assembly" title="London Assembly">London Assembly</a> and STV was used for the newly created <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Assembly" title="Northern Ireland Assembly">Northern Ireland Assembly</a>. The regional <a href="/wiki/Party-list_proportional_representation" title="Party-list proportional representation">party list</a> (<a href="/wiki/Closed_list" title="Closed list">Closed list</a>) system was introduced for European elections in Great Britain (which had previously used single member constituency FPTP) though Northern Ireland continues to use STV.
</p><p>Labour passed the <a href="/wiki/Political_Parties,_Elections_and_Referendums_Act_2000" title="Political Parties, Elections and Referendums Act 2000">Political Parties, Elections and Referendums Act 2000</a>, which created the <a href="/wiki/Electoral_Commission_(UK)" class="mw-redirect" title="Electoral Commission (UK)">Electoral Commission</a>, which since 2000 has been responsible for the running of elections and referendums and to a limited extent regulating party funding. It also reduced the period during which British expatriates can vote, from 20 years after they emigrate to 15.
</p><p>In 2008 the Ministry of Justice delivered a report that failed to conclusively recommend any particular voting system as "best" and instead simply compared working practices used in the different elections. The Minister of State for Justice, Ministry of Justice (<a href="/wiki/Michael_Wills" title="Michael Wills">Michael Wills</a>) issued a statement following its publication stating that no action would be taken on the various reports that, since 1997, have suggested a move towards <a href="/wiki/Proportional_representation" title="Proportional representation">proportional representation</a> for the <a href="/wiki/UK_general_election" class="mw-redirect" title="UK general election">UK general election</a> until reform of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a> is completed.
</p><p>Labour also made many changes to the election administration underpinning the way that elections are run. Changes included postal voting on demand, rolling registration and some innovative pilots such as internet voting<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_issues">Current issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elections_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Current issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electoral_reform">Electoral reform</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elections_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Electoral reform">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Proportional_representation">Proportional representation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elections_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Proportional representation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Alternative_Vote_referendum,_2011" title="United Kingdom Alternative Vote referendum, 2011">United Kingdom Alternative Vote referendum, 2011</a></div>
<p>Some British parties and campaigners have long argued that the current <a href="/wiki/First_Past_the_Post_electoral_system" class="mw-redirect" title="First Past the Post electoral system">First Past the Post system</a> used for <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_elections" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom general elections">Parliamentary elections</a> should be replaced with another system. The introduction of proportional representation has been advocated for some time by the Liberal Democrats, and by some pressure groups such as <a href="/wiki/Charter_88" title="Charter 88">Charter 88</a>, <a href="/wiki/Unlock_Democracy" title="Unlock Democracy">Unlock Democracy</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Electoral_Reform_Society" title="Electoral Reform Society">Electoral Reform Society</a>. In 1998 and 2003 independent commissions were formed to look into electoral reform.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2005" title="United Kingdom general election, 2005">2005 election</a>, in which <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour</a> was elected with the lowest share of the national vote for any single party majority government in British history, more public attention was drawn to the issue. The national newspaper <i><a href="/wiki/The_Independent" title="The Independent">The Independent</a></i> started a petition for the introduction of a more proportional system immediately after the election, under the title "Campaign For Democracy".<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2010" title="United Kingdom general election, 2010">UK 2010 general election</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cameron%E2%80%93Clegg_coalition" title="Cameron–Clegg coalition">the new coalition government</a> agreed to hold a referendum on voting reform. This took place on 5 May 2011: voters were given the choice of switching to the <a href="/wiki/Instant-runoff_voting" title="Instant-runoff voting">Alternative Vote</a> system or retaining the current one. The country voted against AV, with 32% in favour and 68% against.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2015, the non-profit venture <a href="/wiki/Make_Votes_Matter" title="Make Votes Matter">Make Votes Matter</a> was formed to campaign for proportional representation.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> It makes the point that some 68 per cent of votes were ineffective and hence 'wasted' in the UK General Election of 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since February 2016 an anonymous blog, <i>Make Votes Count</i>, seemingly based in the United Kingdom, has intermittently promoted a "First Two Past the Post" voting system.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Parliamentary_and_party_positions">Parliamentary and party positions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elections_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Parliamentary and party positions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>After its inaugural meeting on 29 November 2016 and until September 2017, the All-Party Parliamentary Group on Electoral Reform was a cross-party group consisting of 150 MPs who support electoral reform, chaired by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Burden" title="Richard Burden">Richard Burden</a> and latterly <a href="/wiki/Chuka_Umunna" title="Chuka Umunna">Chuka Umunna</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Labour pledged in its manifesto for the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1997" title="United Kingdom general election, 1997">1997 general election</a> to set up a commission on alternatives to the first-past-the-post system for general elections and to hold a referendum on whether to change the system. The Independent Commission on the Voting System, headed by <a href="/wiki/Roy_Jenkins" title="Roy Jenkins">Lord Jenkins of Hillhead</a> and known as the <a href="/wiki/Jenkins_Commission_(UK)" title="Jenkins Commission (UK)">Jenkins Commission</a>, was established in December 1997. It reported in October 1998 and suggested the <a href="/wiki/Alternative_vote_top-up" class="mw-redirect" title="Alternative vote top-up">Alternative vote top-up</a> or AV+ system.
</p><p>The government had expected a recommendation which could have been implemented within the Parliament, and decided that it would be impractical to have a general election using First Past the Post (FPTP) after a referendum decision to adopt a different system, and therefore delayed the referendum until after the next general election. Those elements within the Labour Party opposed to any change persuaded the party not to repeat the pledge for a referendum in the 2001 manifesto, and therefore none was held once the party was re-elected.
</p><p>After the 2005 election, Lord Chancellor Lord Falconer said there was "no groundswell" for change, although a Cabinet committee was given the task of investigating reform. <a href="/wiki/John_Prescott" title="John Prescott">John Prescott</a> was appointed as Chair; given his known opposition to change, proponents were critical and dismissive of the move. Several prominent Labour MPs expressed a desire for investigating electoral reform, including <a href="/wiki/Peter_Hain" title="Peter Hain">Peter Hain</a> (who argued in the House of Commons in March 2004 for the <a href="/wiki/Instant-runoff_voting" title="Instant-runoff voting">Alternative Vote</a>), <a href="/wiki/Patricia_Hewitt" title="Patricia Hewitt">Patricia Hewitt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tessa_Jowell" title="Tessa Jowell">Tessa Jowell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baroness_Amos" class="mw-redirect" title="Baroness Amos">Baroness Amos</a>.
</p><p>As mentioned above, in January 2008 the government produced a "desk-bound" review of the experience to date of new voting systems in the United Kingdom since Labour came to power in 1997. This review was non-committal as to the need for further reform, especially as regards reform of the voting system used in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_elections_overview" title="United Kingdom general elections overview">Parliamentary Elections</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative Party</a> in the 2005–2010 parliament were predominantly in favour of retaining FPTP. Although the Conservative Party would have won significantly more seats in the 2005 election if some form of proportional representation had been used<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>, some in the party<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (May 2010)">who?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> felt it might find itself politically isolated on the <a href="/wiki/Right_wing" class="mw-redirect" title="Right wing">right</a>, and face Labour/Lib Dem coalition governments. Electoral reform, towards a proportional model, was desired by the Liberal Democrat party, the <a href="/wiki/Green_Party_of_England_and_Wales" title="Green Party of England and Wales">Green party</a>, and several other small parties.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Low_turnout">Low turnout</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elections_in_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Low turnout">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As in many Western democracies, <a href="/wiki/Voter_apathy" title="Voter apathy">voter apathy</a> is a current concern, after a dramatic decline in election turnout around the end of the 20th century. Turnout in UK General Elections fell from 77% in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1992" title="United Kingdom general election, 1992">1992</a>, and 71% in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_General_Election,_1997" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom General Election, 1997">1997</a>, to a historic low of 59% in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_General_Election,_2001" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom General Election, 2001">2001</a>. It has, however, increased since, to 61% in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_General_Election,_2005" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom General Election, 2005">2005</a>, 65% in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_General_Election,_2010" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom General Election, 2010">2010</a>, 66% in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2015" title="United Kingdom general election, 2015">2015</a> and 69% in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2017" title="United Kingdom general election, 2017">2017</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> In other elections turnout trends have been more varied. At the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_independence_referendum,_2014" title="Scottish independence referendum, 2014">referendum on Scottish independence</a> in 2014, turnout exceeded 84.5% - the highest in a large-scale poll since the introduction of universal suffrage - and some local authorities recorded turnouts of over 90%.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Conversely, the <a href="/wiki/Police_and_Crime_Commissioner_elections,_2012" class="mw-redirect" title="Police and Crime Commissioner elections, 2012">Police and Crime Commissioner elections</a> in November 2012 saw a record low turnout of just 15% and the <a href="/wiki/Manchester_Central_by-election,_2012" title="Manchester Central by-election, 2012">Parliamentary by-election in Manchester Central</a> also had a record low peacetime by-election turnout of 18%.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Parliamentary by-election turnout is usually around 30-50%,<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> while local government elections typically see turnouts of around 30% when they are not held alongside higher profile contests such as General or European elections.
</p>

<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.statistics.gov.uk/downloads/theme_other/uk-electoral-statistics-2010.xls">"Office for National Statistics: UK Electoral Statistics 2010"</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. <li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Representation of the People Act 1983, Section 4(5)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As the Representation of the People Act 1983 was enacted after the British Nationality Act 1981, any reference to 'Commonwealth citizen' is defined as a British citizen, a British overseas territories citizen, a British National (Overseas), a British overseas citizen, a British subject or a citizen of a country listed in Schedule 3 of the latter piece of legislation, but <b>not</b> a British protected person.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As the Representation of the People Act 1983 was enacted after the British Nationality Act 1981, any reference to 'Commonwealth citizen' is defined as nationals of countries listed in Schedule 3 of the latter piece of legislation (which includes Fiji and Zimbabwe despite the two countries' current suspension from the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth" title="Commonwealth">Commonwealth</a>).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">including both the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Cyprus" class="mw-redirect" title="Republic of Cyprus">Republic of Cyprus</a> and the semi-recognized <a href="/wiki/Turkish_Republic_of_Northern_Cyprus" class="mw-redirect" title="Turkish Republic of Northern Cyprus">Turkish Republic of Northern Cyprus</a>, but excluding the <a href="/wiki/Akrotiri_and_Dhekelia" title="Akrotiri and Dhekelia">Akrotiri and Dhekelia</a> sovereign base areas.</span>
</li>
